Two Debtors: One Admits Debt Invalid
Intra-Hanafi dispute on joint pawn validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of one creditor's admission of invalidity on a joint pawn, and apply the relied-upon position of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Admission by one joint creditor nullifies only his right, not the entire pawn.

This passage from al-Uyun examines a scenario where two creditors jointly hold a pawn from a common debtor. One creditor later claims the debt was invalid and the pawn a cover-up. The Hanafi masters disagree on whether this voids the entire pawn or only the admitter's share. Understanding this distinction is essential for preserving the rights of innocent parties in joint contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Two creditors each have a claim of 1000 dirhams against one debtor. They jointly take a pawn (land) for both debts and take possession.
2
Examine the admission
One creditor then states that the debt is invalid and the pawn was a talji'a (cover-up).
The admission concerns the validity of both the debt and the pawn.
3
State the two positions
Abu Yusuf: the entire pawn becomes void because it is one contract. Muhammad: only the admitter's right is nullified; the other creditor's right remains intact.
The key difference is whether the pawn is seen as a single right or two separate rights.
4
Apply the preferred ruling
The Hanafi school follows Imam Muhammad's view. The innocent creditor retains his right to the pawn and his claim against the debtor.
This preserves justice for the creditor who did not admit invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في العيون رجلان لكل واحد منهما ألف درهم على رجل فارتهنا منه أرضاً بدينهما وقبضاها ثم قال أحدهما إن المال الذي لنا على فلان باطل والأرض في أيدينا تلجئة قال الفقيه أبو الليث وأبو يوسف رحمه الله : بطل الرهن لأن الدينين وإن اختلفا ولكن الرهن بهما واحد فإذا اعترف أحدهما ببطلان الدين والرهن بطل الرهن أصلاً. وقال محمد الله تعالى : لا يبطل الرهن ويبرأ من حصته من الدين والرهن بحاله لأن الدينين مختلفان والرهن حقاً لهما فإقراره يصح مبطلاً لحق نفسه دون حق شريكه فبطل حق المقر في الدين والرهن وبقي حق الآخر فيهما على حاله
And Abu al-Layth mentioned in al-Uyun: Two men each having one thousand dirhams against a man; they pawned land from him for their debt and took possession. Then one of them said, 'The money we have against so-and-so is invalid, and the land in our hands is a cover-up.' Abu al-Layth and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them) said: The pawn is void, because although the two debts are different, the pawn is one for both. So when one admits the invalidity of the debt and pawn, the pawn becomes completely void. And Muhammad (may Allah Almighty have mercy on him) said: The pawn does not become void; he is discharged from his share of the debt and the pawn remains as it is, because the two debts are different and the pawn is a right for both. So his admission validly nullifies his own right but not his partner's right. So the right of the admitter is nullified in the debt and pawn, and the right of the other remains in both as before.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. They are asked about a situation where two creditors jointly take a pawn from their common debtor. Later, one creditor states that the debt is invalid and the pawn was only a cover (talji'a). Abu Yusuf, supported by Abu al-Layth, holds that the entire pawn becomes void because the pawn is a single contract securing both debts; if one part is null, the whole is null. Imam Muhammad disagrees: the two debts are distinct, and each creditor has a separate right to the pawn. The admission of one only nullifies his own right, leaving the other creditor's right intact. This difference affects whether the remaining creditor can still enforce the pawn. The Hanafi school ultimately follows Imam Muhammad's view, as it preserves the rights of the innocent creditor.

Key Terms
تلجئة (talji'a): A cover-up or simulated transaction to conceal actual ownership

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what happens when one of two joint creditors admits the debt is invalid?
A) The entire pawn becomes void.
B) Both creditors lose their rights equally.
C) The admitting creditor loses his right, the other retains his.
D) The pawn remains valid but the debt is reduced.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, joint creditors have separate rights in a pawn; admission by one only nullifies his own right, not the whole contract.

Indivisibility of Pawn in Joint Debt
Ruling that the entire pawn remains with the mortgagee until all debts are paid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the entire pawn remains with the mortgagee until all debts are paid when one of two joint debtors pays his share.

Core Idea
The pawn is indivisible: partial payment does not release any portion.

When two debtors jointly pawn an item as security for their debts, the pawn is held for the entire debt amount. If one debtor pays his share, the mortgagee does not release any part of the pawn. This lesson clarifies the logic and the analogy to a seller retaining the sold item until full payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and debt
Two debtors owe a debt and pawn a single item to one mortgagee. The debt is shared, but the pawn is one.
2
Recognize indivisibility of possession
The mortgagee's possession of the pawn is not shared; he holds the entire pawn for the entire debt, not proportionally.
This is because possession of the pawn occurs in its entirety from the start.
3
Apply the ruling on partial payment
When one debtor pays his share of the debt, the pawn remains wholly with the mortgagee. No part can be taken back until the full debt is satisfied.
The reasoning: each part of the debt secures the entire pawn, so any remaining debt keeps the whole pawn.
4
Analyze the analogy
The mortgagee is like a seller who retains the sold item until full payment; the debtors are like two buyers jointly buying one item.
This analogy reinforces that partial payment does not release the security.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والمضمون على حصة (دينه لأن كل واحد منهما يصير مستوفياً بالهلاك أحدهما فالكل رهن عند الآخر وكان كله محبوساً بكل جزء من أجزاء الدين فلا يكون له استرداد شيء منه ما دام شيء من الدين باقياً كما إذا كان المرتهن واحداً وكالبائع إذا أدى حصة بعض المبيع فإذا رهن رجلان بدين عليهما رجلاً رهناً واحداً فهو جائز والرهن بكل الدين وللمرتهن أن يمسكه حتى يستوفي جميع الدين لأن قبض الرهن يحصل في الكل من غير شيوع فصار نظير البائع وهما نظير المشتريين
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And what is guaranteed for the share of his debt, because each of them becomes a recipient by perishing.) [Then:] One of them pays his debt, the entire pawn is with the other, and it is all detained by every part of the debt, so he cannot take back anything from it as long as any part of the debt remains, just as if the mortgagee is one, and like the seller when he pays the price of some of the sold item. So if two men owe a debt and they pawn a single pawn to one man, it is permissible and the pawn is for the entire debt, and the mortgagee may hold it until he collects the entire debt, because possession of the pawn occurs in its entirety without sharing, so it is analogous to the seller, and they are analogous to the buyers.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: if there are two debtors who jointly pawn an item for their debts, and one of them pays his share, the entire pawn remains with the mortgagee as security for the unpaid portion of the other debtor. The pawn is held indivisibly for the entire debt, so no part can be released until the full debt is paid. This is analogous to a seller who retains the sold item until full payment. The ruling emphasizes that the mortgagee's possession is not shared; he holds the entire pawn for the entire debt.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Pawn, collateral: an item given as security for a debt.
محبوس (mahbus): Detained, held as security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When one of two joint debtors pays his share of the debt, what happens to the pawn?
A) The pawn is divided proportionally between the debtor and mortgagee.
B) The entire pawn remains with the mortgagee until the full debt is paid.
C) The debtor can take back his share of the pawn.
D) The pawn is released immediately.

Key Takeaway
A jointly pawned item is indivisible: the mortgagee retains it until all debts are cleared.

Conflicting Claims: Two Creditors Each Claim Pawn of Same Slave
Rule on conflicting pawn evidence with qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two creditors present conflicting evidence of a pawn on the same slave and distinguish between the qiyas and istihsan views.

Core Idea
Conflicting claims of full pawn on the same indivisible item cancel each other out; both are invalid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a classic conflict-of-evidence scenario: two creditors each produce witnesses claiming that a specific slave was pawned to them individually. The Hanafi school rules that both claims are invalid because it is logically impossible for one slave to be entirely pawned to two different people at the same time. The passage explains the analogy to sale and notes a potential istihsan exception. Understanding this ruling helps grasp the Hanafi preference for qiyas over istihsan in matters of conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the facts: a man holds a slave, and two creditors each present witness evidence that the slave was pawned to them in full. The slave is currently in the possession of the pawner or a third party.
2
Analyze the Logical Impossibility
Understand that a single indivisible item cannot be simultaneously fully pawned to two different creditors. This impossibility makes the evidence contradictory.
Hanafis consider this a case of mutual cancellation (tahatar).
3
Apply the Qiyas
Analogize to a sale where two buyers each claim full ownership of the same item; such sale is invalid. The effective cause (illa) is the impossibility of complete dual ownership.
Qiyas is the preferred method here.
4
Consider the Istihsan Exception
Some Hanafi scholars suggest joint pawn by istihsan if both creditors have possession and the date is unknown. However, the preferred view (Imam Muhammad) rejects this because each claim stands alone.
The text states 'we take by qiyas'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبطل بينة كل واحد منهما على رجل أنه رهنه عبده وقبضه معناه أن رجلاً في يده عبد وأقام رجلان بينة أنه رهنه العبد الذي في يده فهو باطل لأن كل واحد منهما أثبتت بينته أنه رهنه كل العبد ولا يتصور ذلك لأن العبد الواحد يستحيل أن يكون كله رهنا لهذا وكله رهناً لذاك في حالة واحدة فيمتنع لأحدهما العدم الأولوية ولا وجه إلى القضاء بالنصف لأنه يؤدي إلى الشيوع فتعذر العمل بالبينتين فتهاترتا، ولا يمكن أن يقدر كأنهما ارتهناه معاً استحساناً لجهالة التاريخ لأن ذلك يؤدي إلى العمل بخلاف ما اقتضاء الحجة لأن كلاً منهما أثبت ببيئته حساً يكون وسيلة إلى تملك شطر بالاستيفاء فلا يكون عملاً على وفق الحجة فكان العمل بالقياس أولى لقوة أثره المستتر وهو أن كل واحد منهما أثبت الحق ببينته على حدة ولم يرض بمزاحمة الآخر . قال في العناية : وهو أحد الوجوه في هذه المسئلة وجملتها أن العبد إما أن يكون في أيديهما أو لا أو في يد واحد منهما، فإن كان في يد أحدهما فهو أولى به لأن تمكنه من القبض دليل سبق عنده كما في الشراء كما تقدم إلا أن يقيم الآخر بينة أنه الأول فإنه صريح في السبق وهو يفوق الدلالة وإن لم يكن في يد واحد منهما فهو والمذكور في الكتاب أولاً وكلامه فيه واضح، وإن كان في أيديهما فإن علم الأول منهما فهو أولى ، وإن لم يعلم فهو مسئلة الكتاب على ما ذكر من القياس والاستحسان . قال محمد في الأصل وبه أي بالقياس نأخذ ووجهه ما ذكر في الكتاب ا هـ . أقول : بخلاف ما إذا ارتهنا جملة لأن العقد فيه من جانب الراهن واحد وهنا أثبت كل واحد منهما عقداً آخر والرهن بعقدين مختلفين لا يجوز بخلاف ما لو كان ذلك بعد موت الراهن على ما تبين من الفرق فإذا وقع باطلاً فإذا هلك يهلك أمانة لأن الباطل لا حكم له هذا إذا لم يؤرخا، فإذا أرخا كان به
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the evidence of each of them against a man that he pawned his slave to him is invalid.) Its meaning is that a man has a slave in his hand, and two men present evidence that he pawned the slave in his hand to him. It is invalid because each one's evidence proves that he pawned the entire slave, which is impossible because one slave cannot be entirely pawned to two different people simultaneously. So there is no priority for either, and there is no way to adjudicate half because it would lead to common ownership. So both pieces of evidence are mutually contradictory and cancel out. It cannot be presumed that they pawned it together by istihsan due to unknown date, because that would work contrary to the evidence, since each proved his own right separately and did not accept the other's interference. So acting by qiyas (analogy) is better because of the strength of its hidden effect, which is that each proved his right by his own evidence alone and did not consent to the other's interference. Al-Inayah says: This is one of the aspects of this issue. Summary: The slave is either in the hands of both, or not, or in the hand of one. If in the hand of one, he is more entitled because his possession indicates priority, like in purchase, unless the other brings evidence that he was first, which overtakes the indication. If not in either's hand, it is as mentioned in the book. If in both's hands, if priority is known, that one is entitled; if not, it is the issue of the book as per qiyas and istihsan. Muhammad said in al-Asl: We take by qiyas, and its justification is what is mentioned in the book. I say: Contrary to if they pawned together, because the contract from the pawner is one, but here each proved a separate contract. Pawn by two different contracts is not valid, unlike if it is after the death of the pawner as the difference is clear. So if it is invalid, if it perishes it perishes as a trust, because the invalid has no ruling. This is if they did not date. If they did date, then...
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage deals with a disputed evidence scenario: two creditors each claim that a man pawned the same slave to them individually, and each brings witnesses. The ruling is that both claims are invalid (batal) because it is impossible for the same slave to be fully pawned to two different people at the same time. The text explains that neither has priority, and awarding half is not possible due to indivisibility (shuyu'). The commentary from al-Inayah clarifies that if one of them has possession, he is preferred unless the other proves prior date. If both have possession and priority is unknown, the claims cancel out (tahatarata). The preferred view (of Imam Muhammad) is to follow qiyas (analogy) and declare both invalid. If the claims are dated, the earlier one prevails. This is a classic example of conflicting evidence in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, witness testimony
تهاتر (tahatar): Mutual cancellation of contradicting evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when two creditors each present evidence that the same slave was pawned to them individually?
A) The slave is divided between them equally.
B) The slave goes to the one with prior possession.
C) Both claims are invalid and the slave is returned to the pawner.
D) The slave is sold and proceeds divided.

Key Takeaway
When two claims of full pawn on the same indivisible item conflict without date or possession priority, both are invalid due to logical impossibility.

Dispute over Competing Claims to a Mortgaged Slave
Explains rulings when two creditors claim the same mortgaged slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when two creditors claim exclusive mortgage of the same slave, distinguishing between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf during the mortgagor's life and after his death.

Core Idea
Two proofs cancel during life, but after death each creditor holds half as security.

When two creditors each present evidence that a slave was mortgaged exclusively to them, the Hanafi school must resolve the contradiction. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the detailed analysis, distinguishing between the case during the mortgagor's lifetime and after his death, and contrasting the istihsan-based position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad with the qiyas-based position of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two temporal scenarios
Recognize that the passage first discusses the situation during the mortgagor's life (where the slave is in the hands of neither or one of the creditors) and then after death (where the slave is in the hands of both).
The ruling changes between these two contexts.
2
Analyze the ruling during life
Understand why the two proofs cancel out (tahatur). Since each proof claims exclusive ownership of the entire indivisible slave, they cannot both be true. The slave cannot be fully mortgaged to both, so neither proof prevails.
The impossibility of shuyu' (co-ownership) in rahn during life is key.
3
Examine the qiyas argument for during life
By qiyas (analogy to purchase or marriage claims), the proofs cancel. The hidden effect (athar khafi) is that each creditor established his right separately and cannot share. The stronger position is qiyas over istihsan in this case.
Muhammad al-Shaybani explicitly adopts qiyas in al-Asl.
4
Analyze the ruling after death
After the mortgagor's death, the purpose of mortgage (security) can be achieved through sale of the slave, which is divisible. Thus, each creditor can hold half as security by istihsan (juristic preference).
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad adopt istihsan; Abu Yusuf rejects it.
5
Compare the positions and identify the practical difference
Contrast the thamarah al-khilaf: In life, neither creditor gets a mortgage; the slave remains with the original possessor. After death, each gets a half share as security for his debt, enabling eventual sale and recovery.
The mufta bihi is the istihsan position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

بثبوت ملك المال وهو يقبل الشركة والانقسام. وقوله والعبد في أيديهما وقع اتفاقاً حتى لو لم يكن العبد في أيديهما وأثبت كل واحد فيه الرهن والقبض كان الحكم كذلك ولهذا لم يذكر اليد في المسئلة الأولى فلو تركه هنا لكان أولى والله تعالى أعلم .
One of them, the whole is a mortgage for the other, and the proof of each of them is invalid regarding a man that he mortgaged his slave to him, taking the judgment, and neither is preferred over the other, so it is divided between them because fulfillment [of the debt] occurs in what is divisible. He said in al-'Inayah: from al-Nihayah: an objection is raised that the mortgagee who fulfilled his right completed the purpose of the mortgage, which is a means to actual fulfillment by legal fulfillment, so the mortgage should be in the hand of the other from every aspect without agency on behalf of his companion, and that would require that the mortgagor does not reclaim what he paid to the first of the debt at destruction, but he does reclaim it, and the answer is that the mortgage of each of them remains as long as the mortgage has not reached the mortgagor as we mentioned, and each was fulfilling his debt from half of the property of the mortgage, for it contains payment of both debts, so it appears that the receiver fulfilled his right twice, so he must return what he received a second time. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he pays the debt of one of them, then the whole is a mortgage for the other, and it is all held as security for every part of the debt, so he cannot reclaim any part of it as long as any part of the debt remains, just as if the mortgagee were one, and like the seller when he pays the share of some of the sold item. So if two men mortgage one item to a man for a debt owed to them, it is valid, and the mortgage is for the entire debt, and the mortgagee may hold it until he collects the entire debt because possession of the mortgage occurs over the whole without undivided co-ownership (shuyu'), so it is analogous to the seller, and they are like two buyers. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the proof of each of them regarding a man that he mortgaged his slave to him and took possession is invalid.) Meaning: a man has a slave in his hand, and two men each present proof that he mortgaged to him the slave that is in his hand, so it is invalid because each proof establishes that he mortgaged the entire slave, but that is not possible because a single slave cannot be entirely a mortgage for this one and entirely a mortgage for that one at the same time. So it is impossible for either to have priority, and there is no way to judge by half because it leads to co-ownership (shuyu'), so acting on both proofs is impossible and they cancel each other (tahaturta). It is not possible to estimate that they both mortgaged it together by istihsan due to ignorance of the date because that leads to acting contrary to the implication of the proof, because each establishes by his proof a matter that would be a means to owning a share by fulfillment, so it would not be acting in accordance with the proof, so acting by qiyas is preferable due to the strength of its hidden effect, which is that each established the right by his proof separately and does not accept interference from the other. He said in al-'Inayah: This is one of the aspects in this issue. Its summary: the slave is either in their hands or not, or in the hand of one of them. If it is in the hand of one, he has priority because his possession indicates precedence, as in purchase as previously, unless the other establishes a proof that he is first, for that is explicit in precedence and outweighs the indication. If it is not in the hand of either, then it is as mentioned in the book first, and his speech about it is clear. If it is in their hands, and the first is known, then he has priority; if not known, then it is the issue of the book as mentioned regarding qiyas and istihsan. Muhammad said in al-Asl: and we hold by it, i.e., by qiyas, and its reasoning is as mentioned in the book. End. I say: unlike if they had jointly mortgaged it (irtahana jumlatan) because the contract from the mortgagor is single, while here each establishes a separate contract, and a mortgage with two different contracts is not valid, unlike after the death of the mortgagor as the difference clarifies. So if it is void, then if it is destroyed, it is destroyed as a trust (amanah) because the void has no legal effect. This is if they did not date [their proofs]; if they did date, then the one with the earlier date has priority because he established it at a time when no one disputed him. Similarly, if the mortgage is in the hand of one of them, the possessor has priority because his possession indicates his precedence, like a claim of marriage to a woman or purchase of an item from one, and this has been discussed with its answers previously. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if his mortgagor dies, the slave being in their hands, and each proves as described, then each has half of it as a mortgage for his right.) This is istihsan, and it is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. By qiyas this is void, and it is the view of Abu Yusuf, because the purpose of a mortgage is detention (habs) for fulfillment, which is the primary legal effect of the mortgage contract, so ruling by it is ruling by the mortgage contract, for the effect cannot be established without its cause, and it is void due to co-ownership (shuyu') as in life, and detention of a co-owned property (sha'i') is impermissible, but after death, fulfillment is by sale from its price, and co-owned property can be sold, so it becomes like a claim by two men of marriage to a woman, or two sisters or five women claim marriage to a man: the two proofs cancel each other in life, but we accept them after death because we rule after death on the establishment of ownership of property, which accepts partnership and division. And his statement 'the slave being in their hands' occurred incidentally; even if the slave were not in their hands and each proved both mortgage and possession, the ruling would be the same. For this reason, he did not mention possession (yad) in the first issue, so if he had omitted it here it would have been better, and Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage, a continuation of the chapter on what is permissible for mortgage, presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding competing claims to a mortgaged slave. It opens with the ruling that if one of two creditors is paid, the entire slave remains mortgaged to the other because the slave secures every part of the debt. It then addresses a scenario where two men each present evidence that a slave was mortgaged exclusively to them. According to qiyas (analogy), since each claim asserts full ownership of the indivisible slave, the proofs cancel out (tahatur), and the slave remains with the original possessor. The commentator critiques the possibility of splitting the slave by istihsan, noting that it contradicts the evidence. Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Asl explicitly adopts qiyas. A separate sub-case deals with the situation after the mortgagor's death: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that each creditor holds half of the slave as mortgage by istihsan, because after death the security can be realized through sale of a divisible share. Abu Yusuf opposes, arguing that co-ownership is invalid in mortgage even after death, analogous to marriage claims which are rejected during life but accepted after death. The passage concludes by clarifying that the presence of the slave in the hands of both is incidental; the same ruling applies even if it were not.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is held as security for a debt; the property remains with the creditor until repayment.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
شيوع (shuyu'): Undivided co-ownership; a property owned by multiple persons without physical division.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas to achieve equity or due to necessity.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling if two creditors each prove exclusive mortgage of a slave and the mortgagor dies while the slave is in both their hands?
A) The proofs cancel and the slave goes to the heirs.
B) Each creditor holds half of the slave as mortgage for his debt.
C) The slave is sold and the proceeds divided equally.
D) The creditor with earlier proof takes the whole slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conflicting mortgage claims to an indivisible asset cancel during the debtor's life, but after death each creditor acquires a proportional security interest by istihsan.

The Definition and Role of the Adl in Mortgage Contracts
Definition and conditions of valid adl placement in mortgage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the adl, identify the two conditions for a valid placement of the mortgage with an adl, and distinguish the adl's limited authority as a custodian from any implied authorization to sell.

Core Idea
The adl is a neutral custodian, not an automatic seller — his authority is limited to preservation unless explicitly authorized.

In a mortgage contract, both the mortgagor and mortgagee have interests in the property held as collateral. To avoid conflict, a neutral third party known as the adl (trustworthy person) may be appointed to hold the property. This lesson explains who may serve as adl, what conditions make the placement valid, and whether the adl automatically has the power to sell the property at maturity. Understanding these points is essential to properly structure a mortgage transaction in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The base matn states: 'We placed the mortgage in the hands of a trustworthy person – it is valid.' Confirm the default ruling (sahih) and the simple condition: both parties must be pleased with the adl.
This is the foundational statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Analyze the disputed addition
Some commentators (authors of al-Nihayah and al-'Inayah) added that the parties must also be pleased with the adl selling the mortgage at maturity. Compare this with the view of al-Hakim al-Shahid and the author of al-Hidayah who reject that as a necessary condition.
The dispute is over whether the adl's authority to sell is part of the definition or an extra authorization.
3
Apply the rule to special cases
Examine the examples from al-Ghiyathiyyah: a ma'dhun (authorized slave) cannot stipulate his mortgage be held by his master's freed slave; but the master can validly place his mortgage with his own ma'dhun or mukatib. Understand the reasoning based on capacity and ownership.
The cases illustrate who can validly act as adl based on legal capacity.

باب الرهن يوضع على يد عدل
لما فرغ من الأحكام الراجعة إلى نفس الراهن والمرتهن ذكر هذا الباب الأحكام الراجعة إلى ما بينهما وهو العدل لما أن حكم النائب أبداً يقفو حكم الأصيل. ثم إن المراد بالعدل هنا من رضي الراهن والمرتهن بوضع الرهن في يده. وزاد عليه صاحب النهاية والعناية قيداً آخر حيث قالا : ورضيا ببيعه الرهن عند حلول الأجل. أقول : لعل هذه الزيادة منهما بناء على ما هو الجاري بين الناس فيما هو الغالب وإلا فرضاهما ببيعه الرهن عند حلول الإجل ليس بأمر لازم في معنى العدل وعن هذا قال الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي : ليس للعدل بيع الرهن ما لم يسلط عليه لأنه مأثور بالحفظ فقط .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وضعنا الرهن على يدي عدل صح) ولم يبين المؤلف العدل الذي يصح وضع الرهن على يده والذي لا يصح . في الغياثية : لو شرط المأذون أن يكون رهنه عند مولا ولاه لم يجز، مديوناً كان أو غير مديون. ولو شرط المولى أن يكون رهنه عند عبده المأذون أو المكاتب جاز، ولو شرط أحد شريكي المفاوضة أو العنان
Chapter: Placing the Mortgage in the Hands of a Trustworthy Person (Adl). When he finished from the rulings relating to the mortgagor and mortgagee themselves, he mentioned this chapter on the rulings relating to what is between them, namely the adl, because the ruling of the deputy always follows the ruling of the principal. Then the meaning of adl here is the one with whom both the mortgagor and mortgagee are pleased to place the mortgage in his hand. The author of al-Nihayah and al-'Inayah added another condition, saying: and they are pleased with him selling the mortgage at maturity. I say: perhaps this addition from them is based on what is common among people as the usual practice, otherwise their consent to his selling the mortgage at maturity is not a necessary condition for the meaning of adl. Regarding this, al-Hakim al-Shahid said in al-Kafi: The adl does not have the right to sell the mortgage unless he is authorized, because he is only entrusted with preservation. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (We placed the mortgage in the hands of a trustworthy person (adl) – it is valid.) And the author did not specify which adl is valid to place the mortgage in his hand and which is not. In al-Ghiyathiyyah: If the authorized slave (ma'dhun) stipulates that his mortgage be with his master's freed slave (mawla), it is not valid, whether he is in debt or not. If the master stipulates that his mortgage be with his authorized slave (ma'dhun) or mukatib, it is valid. If one of the partners in a partnership of equal investment (mufawadah) or limited investment ('inan) stipulates...
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on placing the mortgage in the custody of a trustworthy person (adl), who acts as a neutral third party between mortgagor and mortgagee. The author, Imam al-Marghinani, states that the adl is someone agreed upon by both parties to hold the property. The commentators from al-Nihayah and al-'Inayah add that the adl should also be authorized to sell the property at maturity, but the author of al-Hidayah (may Allah have mercy on him) suggests this is not essential, as the adl's primary role is preservation, not sale. Al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi explicitly denies the adl the right to sell without authorization. The passage then gives examples from al-Ghiyathiyyah: if a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) stipulates that his mortgage be held by his master's freed slave, it is invalid; but if the master stipulates that his mortgage be held by his own ma'dhun or mukatib (slave contractually purchasing freedom), it is valid. The text cuts off before completing the discussion on partnership.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): A trustworthy person mutually agreed upon by mortgagor and mortgagee to hold the mortgaged property.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; his capacity is limited in certain contracts.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract of manumission with his master, paying installments for freedom.
نائب (na'ib): Deputy or agent; the adl acts as a deputy for both parties in holding the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To sell the mortgage at maturity.
B) To preserve the mortgage property only.
C) To mediate disputes between the parties.
D) To guarantee the debt.

Key Takeaway
The adl is a custodian, not a seller; his authority to sell requires clear, separate authorization from both parties.

Shared Pledge After Mortgagor's Death: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shared pledge after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that a shared pledge after the mortgagor's death is valid by istihsan and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument based on qiyas.

Core Idea
A shared pledge after death is valid by istihsan because the purpose shifts from detention to sale.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a scenario where a mortgagor dies, leaving a slave as a common pledge between two creditors. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that each creditor holds half as a valid mortgage by istihsan, while Abu Yusuf considers it invalid by qiyas. You will analyze the reasoning behind both positions and understand the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the shared pledge after death is valid (sahih) by istihsan, and each creditor holds half the property as a mortgage.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Recognize that Abu Yusuf opposes this and holds the mortgage invalid (batil) by qiyas.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Sahibayn vs. Abu Yusuf.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's qiyas argument
Understand his reasoning: the essence of mortgage is detention (habs), which is impossible in common ownership; thus the mortgage is invalid by analogy with the lifetime case.
Qiyas: asl (mortgage during life) → hukm (invalid due to shuyu') → illa (inability to detain).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using istihsan
See how the Hanafi position overrides qiyas: after death, satisfaction is via sale, which permits common ownership, so the original illa no longer applies. This is supported by analogy with conflicting marriage claims accepted after death.
Istihsan is a stronger source than qiyas in Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's view, creditors would have no priority; under the relied-upon view, they have a valid mortgage and priority.
This affects the distribution of the deceased's assets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مات راهنه والعبد في أيديهما وبرهن كل واحد على ما وصفنا كان في يد كل واحد منهما نصفه رهنا بحقه. وهذا استحسان وهو قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد في القياس هذا باطل وهو قول أبي يوسف لأن المقصود من الرهن الحبس للاستيفاء وهو الحكم الأصلي لعقد الرهن فيكون الحكم به حكماً بعقد الرهن إذا لا يثبت الحكم بدون علته وأنه باطل بالشيوع كما في حال الحياة والحبس في الشائع لا يقبله وبعد الموت الاستيفاء بالبيع من ثمنه والشائع يقبله فصار كما لو ادعى رجلان نكاح امرأة وادعت أختان أو خمس نسوة النكاح على رجل فإن البينتين يتهاترتان في حالة الحياة، وقبلناها بعد الممات لأنا حكمنا في حالة الموت بثبوت ملك المال وهو يقبل الشركة والانقسام. وقوله والعبد في أيديهما وقع اتفاقاً حتى لو لم يكن العبد في أيديهما وأثبت كل واحد فيه الرهن والقبض كان الحكم كذلك ولهذا لم يذكر اليد في المسئلة الأولى فلو تركه هنا لكان أولى والله تعالى أعلم .
And if his mortgagor dies and the slave is in the hands of both, and each proves a mortgage as we described, then in the hand of each of them half of it is a mortgage for his claim. This is istihsan and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. In qiyas this is invalid, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf, because the purpose of mortgage is detention for recovery, which is the original ruling of the mortgage contract, so ruling by it is a ruling by the mortgage contract, since the ruling is not established without its cause, and it is invalid due to common ownership as in life, and detention in common property is not permissible, but after death recovery is by sale from its price, and common property accepts that. So it becomes like the case where two men claim marriage to a woman, or two sisters or five women claim marriage to a man: the two evidences cancel each other during life, but we accept them after death, because we rule after death by the establishment of ownership of property, which accepts partnership and division. And his statement 'and the slave is in their hands' occurred by chance, so even if the slave were not in their hands and each proved mortgage and possession, the ruling would be the same. That is why he did not mention the hand in the first issue, so leaving it here would have been better. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a scenario where a mortgagor dies and a slave is held as a common pledge between two creditors, each proving a mortgage. The matn states that each creditor holds half of the slave as a mortgage for their respective claim. The commentator explains that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), which is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. In contrast, Abu Yusuf holds that in qiyas (analogy), this is invalid. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that the primary purpose of a mortgage (rahn) is detention (habs) for the satisfaction of the debt. Detention is the original ruling of the contract, and a ruling cannot be established without its cause. During the lifetime of the mortgagor, common ownership (shuyu‘) invalidates the mortgage because detention of a shared property is not possible. However, after death, the satisfaction of the debt is achieved through sale of the property, and sale accepts common ownership. Therefore, istihsan permits it, analogizing to cases where conflicting claims in marriage are reconciled after death. The commentator also notes that the mention of the slave being in their hands is incidental; the ruling holds even if the slave was not physically in their possession. Thus, the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad due to istihsan.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows a ruling to deviate from strict analogy (qiyas) due to a stronger consideration.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause.
رهن (rahn): A pledge or mortgage where property is detained as security for a debt.
شيوع (shuyu'): Common ownership or undivided shares in a property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's ruling that a shared pledge after the mortgagor's death is valid?
A) Qiyas on the validity of mortgages during life
B) Istihsan based on the acceptance of conflicting marriage claims after death
C) Direct textual evidence from Quran and Sunnah
D) Ijma of the companions

Key Takeaway
A shared pledge after the mortgagor's death is valid by istihsan because the purpose shifts from detention to sale, overriding the qiyas objection of common ownership.

Placing a Pledge with a Trustee: Validity and Proof
Expansion on pledge trustee validity with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on placing a pledge with a trustee and explain the qiyas-based proof for its validity.

Core Idea
One hand can legally represent two parties: the trustee's hand is both mortgagor's (trust) and mortgagee's (guarantee).

A pledge secures a debt, but what if both mortgagor and mortgagee agree to place the pledged item with a third party? The Hanafi school holds this valid, using a powerful analogy to the zakah collector. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the definition of a trustee, and the reasoning behind the school's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Opposing View
Start by noting the matn's statement: placing a pledge with a trustee is valid (sahih). Then identify the objection from Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla: the trustee's hand is only the owner's hand, so possession is not transferred.
This step sets up the dispute.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Response: Dual Role of the Trustee's Hand
The Hanafi school argues that the trustee's hand has two aspects: in custody it is the mortgagor's hand (amanah), in financial liability it is the mortgagee's hand (daman). Thus it fulfills both parties' rights.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the Dalil: Qiyas on the Zakah Collector
The text analogizes the trustee to the zakah collector (sa'i). The collector's hand is treated as both the owner's hand (for completing the nisab) and the poor's hand (for sufficiency upon loss). This shows a single hand can serve two legal roles.
The qiyas is the central proof.
4
Apply the Analogy to the Pledge
Just as the zakah collector's hand represents two parties, the trustee's hand represents both mortgagor and mortgagee for different purposes. Therefore, the pledge is valid, and neither party may unilaterally take the item.
This confirms the mufta bihi position.

قال رحمه الله: (ولا يأخذه أحدهما منه) أي من العدل لأنه تعلق به حقها لأن حق الراهن تعلق بالحفظ بيده وأمانته وحق المرتهن في الاستيفاء فلا يملك كل واحد منهما إبطال حق الآخر، ولو شرطا أن يقبضه المرتهن ثم جعلاه على يدي عدل جاز لأن ما جاز للعدل أن
Chapter on Pledge Placed in the Hands of a Trustee. When he finished with the rulings pertaining to the mortgagor and mortgagee themselves, he mentions this chapter on rulings pertaining to what is between them, namely the trustee, because the ruling of the deputy always follows the ruling of the principal. The meaning of trustee here is one with whom both the mortgagor and mortgagee agree to place the pledge. The author of al-Nihayah and al-‘Inayah added another condition, saying: and they consent to his selling the pledge at maturity. I say: perhaps this addition is based on common practice, otherwise their consent to sale at maturity is not necessary for the definition of trustee. For this reason, al-Hakim al-Shahid said in al-Kafi: the trustee may not sell the pledge unless authorized, because he is only entrusted with custody. End quote. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (We placed the pledge in the hands of a trustee, it is valid). The author did not specify which trustee makes the placement valid and which does not. In al-Ghiyathiyyah: if the authorized slave stipulates that his pledge be with his master’s client, it is not permissible, whether he is in debt or not. If the master stipulates that his pledge be with his authorized slave or kitabah slave, it is permissible. If one of the partners of mufawadah or ‘inan, or the mudarib, or the owner of capital stipulates that it be with the other partner, or with the mudarib or owner of capital, it is not permissible. If he buys for his young son and stipulates in the pledge for the price that it be with the father, it is not permissible. If the guarantor gives a pledge and stipulates that it be with the principal, or vice versa, it is permissible. If the pledge is in the hand of an absent trustee who deposited it with someone in his household, then he [the mortgagee] may demand the debt unless the depositee denies the deposit or claims it for himself. If he does not know where it is, the mortgagee swears on his knowledge of loss and takes his debt. If the pledge is in the hands of two trustees, its ruling will come later. The author did not define the trustee. They said in its definition: he is one who is capable of sale, delivery, and collection, whether Muslim, dhimmi, or musta’min harbi as long as he is in our land. If the trustee is not sane, then placing the pledge in his hands is not a pledge because he cannot validly sell, deliver, or collect, so the benefit is lost; as in al-Muhit. It will come later if the trustee is a restricted slave or a minor. Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla said: placement with the trustee is not valid because the trustee’s hand is the owner’s hand; therefore, recourse is to him if the pledge is reclaimed after loss, and after the trustee guarantees its value to the claimant, possession is absent. Our [Hanafi] argument: his hand is the owner’s hand in terms of custody because the property is a trust, and in terms of financial liability it is the mortgagee’s hand because his hand is a hand of guarantee, and what is guaranteed is the financial value. So he is treated as two persons to achieve what they intended, because each of them appointed him, so his hand became like both their hands. For this reason, neither of them may take it from him specifically. If his hand were specifically the hand of one of them, that one could retrieve it from him. It is permissible to treat one hand as two hands; indeed, the zakah collector’s hand is made like the hand of the poor and like the hand of the owner of the wealth, such that if the zakah is lost in his hand, it suffices. If he gives zakah before the year, then the wealth is lost and the year completes despite the loss, the nisab is completed by what is in the collector’s hand as if it were in the owner’s hand, so zakah becomes obligatory on him and he cannot reclaim it. If it were not treated as in the owner’s hand, the nisab would not be complete, and if his hand were not like the poor’s hand, he could reclaim it. The trustee has recourse against the owner for what he guaranteed to the claimant because this guarantee is a guarantee of usurpation, which is established by transfer and conversion; that occurred from the mortgagor and not from the mortgagee, so it is not obligatory on him, unlike the case where the seller and buyer agree to place the sold item in the hand of a trustee, where his hand is only the seller’s hand because making him a deputy of the buyer would contradict the contract’s requirement. The requirement of the sale contract is that the seller’s hand over the sold item is his own hand in terms of both the physical object and the financial value, because he is in no way a deputy of the buyer. When making him a deputy of both is considered, the ruling of sale requires that he be considered a deputy of the seller because the hand originally belonged to him. Not so with pledge, because the physical object is in the mortgagee’s hand as well, and the financial value is guaranteed and it is the mortgagee’s concern, so one person can stand in for both due to the difference of their rights and the lack of a specific contract requirement. Consider: that it is a trust. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And neither of them may take it from him) i.e., from the trustee, because rights are attached to it: the mortgagor’s right is attached to custody in his hand and trust, and the mortgagee’s right is in satisfaction, so neither can annul the other’s right. If they stipulate that the mortgagee take possession and then place it with a trustee, it is permissible because whatever is permissible for the trustee to...

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter of placing a pledge with a trustee. The matn states that placing a pledge with a trustee is valid. The commentary begins by defining the trustee as someone agreed upon by both mortgagor and mortgagee, and notes a difference among scholars whether consent to sale is also required. The author then lists various scenarios regarding who can be a trustee: an authorized slave or master can be trustee in some cases, but partners, mudarib, etc. cannot when stipulated. If the trustee is absent and deposits the pledge with another, the mortgagee may claim the debt. The trustee must be sane and capable of sale/delivery. Then the passage presents an objection from Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla: placement with a trustee is invalid because the trustee's hand is the owner's hand, thus possession is not transferred to the mortgagee, making the pledge ineffective. The Hanafi response, using qiyas (analogy with the zakah collector), argues that the trustee's hand serves dual purposes: in custody it represents the mortgagor (as a trust), and in financial liability it represents the mortgagee (as a guarantee). Thus, the trustee's hand is like two hands. The analogy with the zakah collector shows that a single hand can represent two parties for different legal purposes. Consequently, the pledge is valid, and neither party can unilaterally take the pledge from the trustee because both have rights attached to it. If the trustee is sued by a third party claiming ownership, the trustee can have recourse against the mortgagor. The passage concludes with the ruling that neither mortgagor nor mortgagee may take the pledge from the trustee without the other's consent; even if the mortgagee initially had possession and then placed it with a trustee, it is valid.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A trustee agreed upon by mortgagor and mortgagee to hold the pledge, capable of sale and delivery.
يد (yad): Legal hand or possession; can be of various types: amanah (trust), daman (guarantee), etc.
ساعي (sa'i): Zakah collector appointed by the state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why is placing a pledge with a trustee valid?
A) The trustee is a neutral third party
B) The trustee's hand is like the zakah collector's hand, representing both parties
C) The mortgagee can take possession at any time
D) The mortgagor retains full ownership

Key Takeaway
A trustee's hand in a pledge legally acts as both the mortgagor's hand (for custody) and the mortgagee's hand (for financial liability), making the pledge valid.

The Trustee in Pledge: Definition and Validity
Defines trustee and rebuts Zufar's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid trustee in a pledge contract and explain why the Hanafi school holds that placement with a trustee gives possession to the mortgagee.

Core Idea
The trustee's hand is both the pledgor's trust and the pledgee's guarantee, making the pledge valid.

When a pledge is placed with a third party, the mortgagee must obtain possession for the contract to be binding. Zufar argued that a trustee's hand is only the owner's hand, so possession is not achieved. The Hanafi school disagrees, offering a sophisticated theory of dual representation. This lesson unpacks that theory and its supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Define the trustee
Identify the necessary qualifications: capable of selling, paying, and receiving; can be Muslim, dhimmi, or musta'min; must be sane.
If the trustee is insane or a restricted minor, placement is invalid because he cannot perform the necessary transactions.
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla argue that the trustee's hand is solely the hand of the owner (rahin), so the mortgagee does not obtain possession. Therefore, the pledge is invalid.
This is based on the idea that the trustee is only a custodian for the owner.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The trustee's hand is dual: it acts as the rahin's hand for custody (trust) and the murtahin's hand for financial liability (guarantee). This is possible because the rights are different.
The guarantee attaches to the financial value, not the physical object.
4
Examine the zakat analogy
The zakat collector's hand is treated as the hand of both the poor (for receiving) and the owner (for preserving the nisab). This precedent shows one hand can represent two parties.
If zakat perishes in the collector's hand, it suffices; if not for dual representation, the nisab would be incomplete.
5
Distinguish from sale
In a sale, a trustee's hand is only the seller's hand because the seller retains possession until delivery. Pledge differs because the object's financial value is transferred to the mortgagee while physical custody remains with the pledgor via the trustee.
The cause of the contract dictates the hand's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يعرف المؤلف العدل قالوا في تعريفه : هو الذي يقدر على البيع والايفاء والاستيفاء مسلماً كان أو ذمياً أو حربياً مستأمناً ما دام في دارنا، فلو كان العدل غير عاقل فموضع الرهن على يديه لم يكن رهناً لأنه لم يصح منه البيع والايفاء والاستيفاء فلغا الفائدة؛ كذا في المحيط. وسيأتي لو كان العدل عبداً محجوراً أو صبياً. وقال زفر عن وابن أبي ليلى : لا يصح الوضع عند العدل لأن يد العدل يد المالك ولهذا يرجع إليه إذا استحق الرهن بعد الهلاك وبعدما ضمن العدل قيمته بما ضمن المستحق فانعدم القبض. ولنا أن يده يد المالك في الحفظ لكون العين أمانة وفي حق المالية يد المرتهن لأن يده يد ضمان والمضمون هو المالية فنزل منزله شخصين لتحقق ما قصداه لأن كلاً منهما أمره فصارت يده كيدهما، ولهذا لا يكون لأحدهما أن يأخذ منه على الخصوص. ولو كانت يده يد أحدهما على الخصوص كان له أن يسترده منه، ويجوز أن يجعل اليد الواحدة في حكم يدين ألا الساعي جعلت يده كيد الفقير وكيد صاحب المال حتى إذا هلكت الزكاة في يده أجزأته. ولو قدم الزكاة قبل الحول فانتقض المال وتم الحول على التناقض يتم النصاب بما في يد الساعي كأنه في يد المالك فتجب عليه الزكاة ولا يملك استرداده، ولو لم يجعل كأنه في يد المالك لم يتم النصاب، ولو لم يجعل يده كيد الفقير لملك استرداده وإنما يرجع العدل على المالك بما ضمن للمستحق لأن هذا الضمان ضمان الغصب وذلك يتحقق بالنقل والتحويل ووجد ذلك من الراهن ولم يوجد من المرتهن فلا يجب عليه بخلاف ما إذا أنفق البائع والمشتري على وضع المبيع في يد عدل حيث تكون يده يد البائع فحسب لأن في جعله نائباً عن المشتري يعتبر موجباً للعقد، فإن موجب عقد المبيع أن تكون يد البائع على المبيع يد نفسه في حق العين والمالية جميعاً لأنه ليس بنائب عن المشتري بوجه ما. وإذا كان في جعله نائباً عنهما يعتبر حكم البيع اعتبر نائباً عن البائع لأن اليد كانت له في الأصل ولا كذلك الرهن لأن عينه في يده بل في يد المرتهن أيضاً والمالية هي ! المضمونة وهي في حق المرتهن فأمكن أن يقوم شخص واحد مقامهما لاختلاف حقهما فيه وعدم تعيين موجبه .
And the author did not define 'adl. They said in its definition: He is one who is capable of selling, paying, and receiving, whether Muslim, dhimmi, or musta'min harbi as long as he is in our abode. If the 'adl is not sane, the placement of the pledge in his hands is not a valid pledge because selling, paying, and receiving are not valid from him, so the benefit is void; likewise in al-Muhit. It will come later if the 'adl is a restricted slave or a minor. And Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla said: Placement with the 'adl is not valid because the hand of the 'adl is the hand of the owner, and therefore he returns to him when the pledge is claimed after destruction and after the 'adl has guaranteed its value by what he guaranteed to the claimant, so possession is nullified. And our proof: That his hand is the hand of the owner in custody because the object is a trust, and in terms of financial liability it is the hand of the mortgagee because his hand is a hand of guarantee, and the guaranteed thing is the financial value. So he is treated as two persons to achieve what both intended, because each of them entrusted him, so his hand became as their hand. Hence neither of them may take from him exclusively. And if his hand were the hand of one of them exclusively, that one could take it back from him. And it is permissible to treat one hand as two hands; indeed, the zakat collector's hand is made like the hand of the poor and like the hand of the owner of the wealth, so that if the zakat perishes in his hand, it suffices. And if he gave zakat before the haul, and the wealth is diminished, and the haul completes on the diminished state, the nisab is completed by what is in the collector's hand as if it were in the owner's hand, so zakat becomes obligatory on him and he cannot take it back. And if it were not treated as in the owner's hand, the nisab would not be complete, and if his hand were not like the hand of the poor, he would own its return. And the 'adl only seeks recourse against the owner for what he guaranteed to the claimant because this guarantee is a guarantee of usurpation, which is realized by transfer and conversion, and that occurred from the rahin and not from the murtahin, so it is not obligatory on him. Unlike when the seller and buyer agree on placing the sold item in the hand of an 'adl, where his hand is only the hand of the seller, because making him a deputy of the buyer would require a contract cause. Indeed, the cause of the sale contract requires that the seller's hand on the sold item be his own hand regarding the object and the financial value all together, because he is not a deputy of the buyer in any way. And when making him a deputy for both is considered based on the ruling of sale, he is considered a deputy for the seller because the hand originally belonged to him. This is not so in pledge because the object is in his hand, rather also in the hand of the murtahin, and the financial value is the guaranteed thing, and it is regarding the murtahin. So it is possible for one person to stand in place of both due to the difference of their rights in it and the absence of a specific cause for its requirement.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition and qualifications of the trustee ('adl) in a pledge contract, and the validity of placing the pledged property with him. The commentator first notes that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not define 'adl. He then provides a definition from other scholars: the 'adl must be capable of selling, paying, and receiving; he may be Muslim, dhimmi, or musta'min harbi as long as he is in Islamic territory. If he is insane, the placement is invalid because he lacks capacity for the necessary transactions. The passage also mentions that a restricted slave or a minor will be discussed later. Then it presents the objection of Zufar and Ibn Abi Layla, who argue that placing the pledge with a third party is invalid because the trustee's hand is deemed the hand of the owner (the rahin), and therefore possession by the murtahin is not established. The Hanafi response follows: the trustee's hand serves a dual function—as the hand of the rahin for safekeeping (since the property is a trust) and as the hand of the murtahin for financial liability (since the murtahin has a guarantee interest). This is possible because the two parties have different rights in the pledge. The commentator supports this with an analogy to the zakat collector, whose hand is considered both the hand of the poor (regarding entitlement) and the hand of the owner (regarding possession for zakat calculation). The passage then distinguishes the case of a sale contract, where a third-party trustee's hand is only the seller's hand because the contract's nature requires the seller to retain possession. Thus, the Hanafi position validates placement with a trustee, rebutting Zufar's objection.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A trustworthy third party appointed to hold the pledged property; must be capable of selling, paying, and receiving.
يد (yad): Possession or control; in this context, the legal status of the trustee's hand as representing both parties.
أمانة (amanah): Trust; the property in the trustee's hand is a trust from the rahin, so the trustee is not liable for loss unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the trustee's hand serves as both the hand of the pledgor (custody) and the hand of the pledgee (guarantee).
C) Because the trustee is an agent of the court.
D) Because the pledgee directly possesses the property through the trustee.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates placement with a qualified trustee because the trustee's hand can simultaneously represent the pledgor's trust and the pledgee's guarantee, meeting the needs of both parties.

Trustee Liability in Pledge Delivery
Rules on trustee actions and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the trustee becomes liable when delivering the pledge, and identify the rights of the pledgor and pledgee.

Core Idea
The trustee (adl) is liable if he delivers the pledge without authorization; he holds it for both parties' rights.

This passage explains the delicate role of the trustee (adl) in a pledge contract. The adl is entrusted with the property to preserve both the pledgor's right of custody and the pledgee's right of retention. Any unauthorized delivery triggers liability. We will examine when the adl is liable, the effect of stipulations, and how compensation is handled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
The text begins by stating that neither party may take the pledge from the adl. Understand why: both have attached rights.
The pledgor has a right to safekeeping, the pledgee to retention.
2
Analyze the exception for stipulation
If they stipulate that the pledgee takes possession and then place it with an adl, it is valid. This shows that the adl can stand in initially and continuously.
3
Determine liability on delivery
If the adl delivers to the pledgor or pledgee without proper authorization, he becomes liable. Examine both cases: delivering to pledgee is like delivering to a stranger; delivering to pledgor nullifies the pledgee's retention right.
4
Understand the compensation scenario
When the adl pays the value and it is made a new pledge, follow the rights after debt payment: if liability was for delivering to pledgor, pledgor cannot claim value; if for delivering to pledgee, pledgor can claim.
5
Distinguish recourse against pledgee
If the adl delivered as a loan/deposit, no recourse; if delivered as a pledge (saying 'take this pledge'), recourse is possible if the pledge perishes.
The nature of the delivery determines liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يأخذه أحدهما منه) أي من العدل لأنه تعلق به حقها لأن حق الراهن تعلق بالحفظ بيده وأمانته وحق المرتهن في الاستيفاء فلا يملك كل واحد منهما إبطال حق الآخر، ولو شرطا أن يقبضه المرتهن ثم جعلاه على يدي عدل جاز لأن ما جاز للعدل أن يقوم مقام المرتهن في الابتداء فكذلك في البقاء. ولو دفع العدل الرهن إلى الراهن أو المرتهن يضمن لأنه متى دفع إلى المرتهن فقد دفع الأمانة بغير إذنه كما لو دفع إلى أجنبي ومتى دفع إلى الراهن فقد أبطل ملك اليد والحبس على المرتهن فإنه يثبت له ملك اليد والحبس بقبض العدل، وإبطال ملك السيد كإبطال ملك العين في ايجاب الضمان، فإن من أتلف الرهن يضمن للمرتهن كما يضمن للراهن وإن قبضا القيمة من العدل وجعلاها رهناً في يد العدل ثم قضى الراهن دين المرتهن فأراد أن يأخذ القيمة من العدل ينظر ؛ إن كان العدل ضمن بدفع الرهن إلى الراهن ليس له ذلك لأنه وصل إليه حقه فتبقى القيمة للعدل، وإن كان ضمن بدفع الرهن إلى المرتهن والراهن أخذ القيمة منه لأنه لو كان الرهن قائماً بعينه في يده بعد قضاء الدين فللراهن أخذه وكذلك أخذ بدله . ثم العدل هل يرجع بالقيمة على المرتهن ينظر؛ إن دفع الرهن إليه على وجه العارية والوديعة لا يرجع بقيمة ما دفع إليه إن كان هلك الرهن في يد المرتهن إن العدل لما ملك القيمة فقد ملك الرهن بالضمان فصار معيراً ومودعاً ملكه، فإن دفع إليه رهناً بأن قال خذ هذا رهنك خذه فاحسبه يرجع العدل عليه بالقيمة لو هلك يده لأنه ملكه بأداء الضمان وقد دفع إلى المرتهن بجهة مضمونة وهي الرهن فصار كما لو دفعه إليه على سوم القرض والبيع وهذه التعريفات ذكرها الفقيه أبو جعفر الهنداوي رحمه الله تعالى.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And neither of the two takes it from him,' i.e., from the 'adl, because a right is attached to it: the right of the pledgor is attached to its preservation in his hand as a trust, and the right of the pledgee is in collection, so neither can nullify the other's right. And if they stipulate that the pledgee takes possession and then place it with an 'adl, it is permissible, because what is permissible for the 'adl to stand in place of the pledgee initially, likewise in continuation. And if the 'adl delivers the pledge to the pledgor or the pledgee, he is liable. Because when he delivers to the pledgee, he has delivered the trust without his permission, just as if he delivered to a stranger. And when he delivers to the pledgor, he has nullified the right of possession and retention for the pledgee, because the right of possession and retention is established for him through the 'adl's taking. And nullifying the right of possession is like nullifying the right over the object in imposing liability. For whoever destroys the pledge is liable to the pledgee just as he is liable to the pledgor. And if they both take the value from the 'adl and make it a pledge in his hand, then the pledgor pays the pledgee's debt and wants to take the value from the 'adl, it is considered: if the 'adl was liable due to delivering the pledge to the pledgor, he does not have that (right) because the pledgor has received his right, so the value remains for the 'adl. And if he was liable due to delivering to the pledgee, the pledgor takes the value from him because if the pledge itself had remained in his hand after paying the debt, the pledgor could take it, and likewise its substitute. Then, does the 'adl have recourse against the pledgee for the value? It is considered: if he delivered the pledge to him as a loan or deposit, he does not have recourse for the value of what he delivered if the pledge perishes in the hand of the pledgee, because the 'adl, when he owned the value, he owned the pledge by guarantee, so he became a lender and depositor of his own property. But if he delivered to him a pledge saying, 'Take this pledge of yours, take it and count it,' then the 'adl has recourse against him for the value if it perishes in his hand, because he owned it by paying the guarantee and delivered it to the pledgee in a guaranteed capacity (as a pledge), so it is as if he delivered it to him on the basis of a loan or sale. And these definitions were mentioned by faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindawi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage contains several rulings regarding the trustee ('adl) in a pledge. First, the matn states that neither the pledgor nor the pledgee may take the pledged property from the 'adl. The commentator explains why: both parties have rights in the pledge—the pledgor has a right to custody and safekeeping, and the pledgee has a right to collect from it. If one party takes it unilaterally, the other's right is nullified. If they both agree to allow the pledgee to take possession initially and then place it with an 'adl, it is valid because the 'adl can stand in for the pledgee throughout. Next, if the 'adl delivers the pledge to either party without authorization, he becomes liable. Delivering to the pledgee is like delivering to a stranger because the 'adl is a trustee; delivering to the pledgor destroys the pledgee's right of possession and retention. The passage then discusses scenarios where the 'adl pays compensation for the pledge and it is made into a new pledge, and what happens after the debt is paid. The 'adl's recourse against the pledgee depends on the nature of the delivery: if it was as a loan or deposit, no recourse; if as a pledge, the 'adl can claim its value. The commentator credits these detailed rules to faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindawi.

Key Terms
يد الحبس (yad al-habs): Right of retention; the pledgee's right to hold the pledged property until the debt is paid.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the 'adl becomes liable if he improperly delivers the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the pledgee damages it.
B) Always, because delivering to the pledgee is like delivering to a stranger without permission.
C) Only if the pledgor objects.
D) Never, because the pledgee has a right to it.

Key Takeaway
The 'adl is a dual trustee; unauthorized delivery to either party triggers liability, and the nature of delivery determines subsequent recourse.

Two Trustees and Divisible Pledge
Ruling on two trustees and custody of divisible pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on two trustees with divisible pledge and identify the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Tahayu' permits one trustee to hold divisible property without liability.

When a pledged property is divisible and two trustees are appointed, joint custody at all times may be impractical. The Hanafi school allows rotation (tahayu') without liability, but for indivisible property, the receiver is liable by consensus, and Abu Hanifa also holds the deliverer liable, contrary to the Sahibayn. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the internal difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine whether the pledged property is divisible or indivisible. The ruling hinges on this classification.
Divisible: can be split into parts; indivisible: cannot.
2
Understand tahayu'
Recognize that joint custody is impossible for divisible property, so rotation is allowed. The permission to one trustee is inferred from the impossibility.
Thābit dalālatan ka al-thābit naṣṣan.
3
Note the consensus on receiver's liability
For indivisible property, the trustee who receives the pledge is liable by consensus (ijma').
This is explicit: 'yadman al-qābiḍ bi al-ijmā''.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's additional liability
Abu Hanifa holds the delivering trustee also liable, based on analogy to deposits (wadi'ah).
The deliverer is considered to have transgressed.
5
Contrast with the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad only impose liability on the receiver; the deliverer is not liable.
This is the 'khilāf' mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان العدل رجلين والرهن مالاً يقسم فوضعاه عند أحدهما جاز ولم يضمنا لأن اجتماعهما على حفظ جميع الرهن في الأوقات كلها وهو لا يقسم متعذر فلم يبق إمكان الحفظ إلا بالتهايؤ ومطلق الأمر بالحفظ يتصرف إلى حفظ يمكن بدلالة إحالة الأمر وذلك بالتهايؤ، والثابت دلالة كالثابت نصاً فجعل الدفع إلى أحدهما بإذن المالك فلم يضمنا. وإن كان مما يقسم يضمن القابض بالإجماع ويضمن الدافع عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى خلافاً لهما على ما عرف في الوديعة. قال رحمه الله : ( ويهلك في ضمان المرتهن) لأن يده في حق المالية يد المرتهن والمالية هي المضمونة ولو دفع العدل الرهن إلى أحدهما ضمن لأنه مودع الراهن في حق العين ومودع المرتهن في حق المالية وكل منهما أجنبي عن الآخر والمودع يضمن بالدفع إلى الاجنبي وإذا ضمن العدل قيمة الرهن بالتعدي فيه إما بإتلافه أو بدفعه إلى أحدهما وأتلفه المدفوع إليه لا يقدر العدل أن يجعل القيمة رهناً في يده لأن القيمة واجبة عليه فلو جعلها رهناً في يد يصير قاضياً ومقتضياً وبيتهما تناف ولكن يأخذانها منه ويجعلانها رهناً عنده أو عند غيره فيجوز، فإن تعذر اجتماعهما يرفع أحدهما الأمر إلى القاضي ليفعل ذلك، فإن جعل القيمة رهناً برأيهما أو برأي القاضي عند العدل الأول أو عند غيره ثم قضى الراهن الدين فقد تقدم بيانه قال رحمه الله : (فإن وكل الراهن المرتهن أو العدل أو غيرهما ببيعه عند حول الدين صح لأن الراهن مالك فله أن يوكل من ماله مطلقاً ومنجزاً من الأهل ببيع
And if the trustees are two men and the pledge is property that can be divided, and they place it with one of them, it is permissible and they are not liable, because their joint custody of all the pledge at all times is difficult when it is divisible, so there is no possibility of custody except by rotation (tahayu'). And the absolute command to preserve is interpreted as preservation that is possible, by indication of the matter's impossibility, so the established by indication is like established by text, so the delivery to one is by permission of the owner, so they are not liable. But if it is not divisible, the receiver is liable by consensus, and the one who delivered is liable according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), contrary to his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), as is known in deposits. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And it perishes in the guarantee of the mortgagee.' Because his hand regarding financial value is the hand of the mortgagee, and the financial value is the guaranteed thing. And if the 'adl delivers the pledge to one of them, he is liable because he is a depositary of the pledgor regarding the object and a depositary of the mortgagee regarding the financial value, and each of them is a stranger to the other, and a depositary is liable by delivering to a stranger. And when the 'adl becomes liable for the value of the pledge through transgression, either by destroying it or delivering it to one of them and the recipient destroys it, the 'adl cannot make the value a pledge in his own hand because the value is obligatory upon him; if he made it a pledge in his hand, he would become both the debtor and the creditor, which are contradictory. But they both take it from him and make it a pledge with him or with another, and that is permissible. And if they cannot both meet, one of them refers the matter to the judge to do that. And if the value is made a pledge with their agreement or by the judge's opinion with the first 'adl or another, then the pledgor pays the debt, and its explanation has preceded. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And if the pledgor appoints the mortgagee, the 'adl, or someone else to sell it at the maturity of the debt, it is valid because the pledgor is the owner, so he has the right to appoint a deputy over his property absolutely and unconditionally from the family to sell.'
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct rulings. First, if there are two trustees and the pledged property is divisible, they may place it with one of them and are not liable, because joint custody at all times is impractical. The permission for one to hold it is implied by the situation. If the property is not divisible, the one who receives it is liable by consensus, and the one who delivers it is liable according to Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ (they hold only the receiver liable). Second, the matn states that the pledge perishes in the guarantee of the mortgagee. The commentator explains that this is because the trustee's hand is the hand of the mortgagee regarding the financial value, which is guaranteed. If the trustee delivers the pledge to either party, he becomes liable. The value he pays cannot be made a pledge in his own hand because that would merge debtor and creditor. Instead, the parties or the judge can make it a pledge with another. Third, the pledgor may validly appoint the mortgagee, the trustee, or another person to sell the pledge at the debt's maturity, because the pledgor owns the property and can delegate sale.

Key Terms
تهايؤ (tahayu'): Rotation or division of duties among trustees; a practical arrangement for custody of divisible property.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression or misuse of the pledged property by the trustee, triggering liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when two trustees place a divisible pledge with one of them?
A) It is invalid and trustees are liable.
B) It is permissible and trustees are not liable.
C) It is permissible but the trustees are liable.
D) It is invalid but trustees are not liable.

Key Takeaway
For divisible property, trustees may rotate custody without liability; for indivisible, only the receiver is liable by consensus, and the deliverer is also liable per Abu Hanifa.

Perishing of Pledge and Trustee's Liability
Ruling on perishing of pledge and trustee's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the pledge perishes in the guarantee of the mortgagee and explain the trustee's liability when delivering to a stranger.

Core Idea
The trustee's hand is the mortgagee's hand regarding value; delivery to a stranger makes the trustee liable.

When a pledge perishes, the legal question is who bears the loss. The Hanafi school holds that the pledge perishes in the guarantee of the mortgagee because his hand controls the financial value. However, if the trustee (adl) delivers the pledge to either party, he becomes liable for transgression. This lesson explains the reasoning and the rule that the value cannot be made a pledge in the trustee's own hand.

Guided Path
1
Understand the phrase 'يهلك في ضمان المرتهن'
This means the pledge perishes at the risk of the mortgagee; he is liable for its value if it perishes in his custody.
The mortgagee's hand regarding financial value is controlling.
2
Identify the trustee's role
The trustee is a depositary (mustawda') of both parties: as to the physical object for the pledgor, and as to the financial value for the mortgagee.
Thus he is an agent for both.
3
Determine liability upon delivery to one party
If the trustee delivers the pledge to either the pledgor or the mortgagee without authorization, he becomes liable because the other is a stranger.
The trustee becomes a guarantor (dāmin) for the value.
4
Examine the consequence of transgression
If the trustee's transgression leads to the pledge's destruction, he owes its value. He cannot make that value a new pledge in his own hand because that would merge debtor and creditor.
The parties or the judge must arrange a new pledge with a neutral party.
5
Follow the procedure
The parties jointly take the value from the trustee and make it a pledge with him or another, or the judge does it if they cannot agree.
The text says 'يأخذانها منه ويجعلانها رهناً عنده أو عند غيره'.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the trustee transgesses and owes the value of the pledge?
A) He can immediately make the value a new pledge in his own hand.
B) He must pay the value to the parties, who may then make it a pledge with him or another.
C) The pledge contract is void.
D) The mortgagee loses his right.

Key Takeaway
The pledge perishes in the mortgagee's guarantee; the trustee becomes liable if he delivers to a stranger, and the owed value cannot be re-pledged with himself.
